The spacecraft was manned by Captain Eugene A.\nCernan, Commander; Commander Ronald E. Evans, Command Module P i l o t ; and\nD r . Harrison H. Schmitt, Lunar Module P i l o t .\nThe launch countdown had proceeded smoothly u n t i l T minus 30 seconds,\nat which time a f a i l u r e i n t h e automatic countdown sequencer occurred and\ncaused a launch delay of 2 hours and 40 minutes. This w a s the only launch\ndelay i n the Apollo program t h a t was ceilsed by hardware f a i l u r e . A s a, result , t h e launch azimuth w a s adjusted a d an e a r t h parking o r b i t of 92.5miles by 91.2-miles w a s achieved. The vehicle remained i n e a r t h o r b i t\nf o r approximately 3 hours before t h e t r v l s l u n a r i n j e c t i o n mmeuver was\ni n i t i a t e d . The t r a n s l u h a r coast time wzs shortened t o compensate f o r t h e\nlaunch delay. Transposition, docking, cnd lunar module e j e c t i o n were norm a l . The S-IVB s t a g e was maneuvered for lunar impact , which occurred\nabout 84 miles from t h e pre-planned poizt. The impact w a s recorded by\nt h e Apollo 1 2 , 14, 15 , and 1 6 passive seismometers.\n\n\\I\n\nThe crew performed a heat flow and convection demonstration and an\nApollo l i g h t f l a s h experiment during the translunar coast period. About\nfour hours l a t e r , t h e spacecraft o r b i t was lowered t o one having a 59-mile\napocynthion and a 14.5-mile pericynthion. After spending 1 7 hours i n t h i s\nlower o r b i t , t h e command and service module separated from t h e lunar modu l e a f t e r which t h e command and service module o r b i t was circularized i n t o\none having a 70-mile apocynthion and a 54-mile pericynthion.\n3.3.2\n\nDescent\n\nFive minutes after t h e c i r c u l a r i z a t i o n maneuver w a s i n i t i a t e d by t h e\ncommand and service module, t h e lunar module performed t h e second descent\no r b i t i n s e r t i o n maneuver. This lowered i t s pericynthion t o within 6.2 m i l e s of t h e lunar surface. A hour l a t e r , the lunar module powered descent\nn\nwas i n i t i a t e d and t h e lunar module landed on t h e moon a t 110 hours 21 minutes 58 seconds. A manual t a r g e t update of 3400 f e e t w a s incorporated\ne a r l y i n t h e powered descent. Later i n the descent maneuver, t h e Commander\nmade e i g h t landing point redesignations. These redesignations r e s u l t e d i n\nt h e spacecraft landing at 20 degrees 9 minutes 55 seconds north l a t i t u d e\nand 30 degrees 45 minutes 57 seconds east longitude on t h e 1:25 000-scale\nL u n a r Topographic Photomap of Taurus Littrow, F i r s t Edition, September,\n1972.\n\n3.3.3\n\nAscent and Rendezvous\n\nThe planned decay of t h e command and service module a l t i t u d e t o\nmatch t h e l u n a r module t r a j e c t o r y at rendezvous w a s not realized. This\nwas similar t o t h e experience of t h e Apollo 1 5 mission. Because of t h i s ,\nan o r b i t a l t r i m maneuver was performed t o change t h e command and service\nmodule apocynthion t o 67.3 miles and the pericynthion t o 62.5 miles. A\nn\nhour l a t e r , a plane change maneuver was perforned t o provide the proper\no r b i t a l plane f o r rendezvous with t h e lunar w d u l e .\nThe lunar module ascended from the lunar surface at 185 hours 2 1 minutes 37 seconds after having been on the lunar surface f o r almost 75 hours.\nApproximately 7 1 / 2 minutes l a t e r , t h e ascent stage was i n s e r t e d i n t o lun a r o r b i t . The achieved o r b i t required a vernier adjustment maneuver of\n1 0 f t / s e c t o r e t u r n t h e o r b i t t o the planned conditions f o r rendezvous.\nThe rendezvous w a s then completed normally, and t h e two vehicles were\ndocked at 187 hours 37 minutes 1 5 seconds.\n\n_.\n\nI\n1\n\n.._-_\n-\n\n-.\n\n.... _ - - . .\n\n.\n\n. _\n\n,..\n\n..\n\n,\'\n\n..\n\n3-9\n\n3.3.4\n\nLunar Module Deorbit Maneuver\n\nThe lunar module was j e t t i s o n e d four hours af\'ter docking. The lunar\nmodule deorbit maneuver began about an hour and a h a l f a f t e r j e t t i s o n i n g\nand impact occurred at 19 degrees 57 minutes 58 seconds north l a t i t u d e ,\nand 30 degrees 29 minutes 23 seconds east longitude, about 9.9 kilome t e r s from t h e Apollo 17 landing s i t e , and about 1.75 kilometers from\nt h e planned impact point ( f i g s . 3-1 and 4-1).\n\n3.4\n\nT A S A T AND ENTRY TRAJECTORY\nR NE R H\n\nThe command and service module reaained i n lunar o r b i t approximately\n43 hours a f t e r t h e lunar module w a s jettisoned. The t r a n s e a r t h i n j e c t i o n\nmaneuver was i n i t i a t e d at 234 hours 2 minutes 9 seconds. The maneuver\nw a s so accurate t h a t only one midcourse correction w a s required during\nt r a n s e a r t h c o a s t , and t h a t was at three hours p r i o r t o entry with a d i f f e r e n t i a l v e l o c i t y of 2 . 1 f t / s e c .\nThe command and service modules were separated 1 5 minutes before\ne n t r y i n t o t h e e a r t h \' s atmosphere. The command module entered the atmosphere 1200 miles from t h e landing point and the landing occurred\n1.3 miles short of the; t a r g e t e d point. The e a r t h landing coordinates,\nas determined from t h e spacecraft computer, were 17 degrees 52 minutes\n48 seconds south l a t i t u d e and 166 degrees 6 minutes 36 seconds west\nlongitude.\n\n_.\n\nc\n\n.\n\n.\n\n_i..\n\n.\n\n.\n\n. . . ,.\n\n.\n. ...\n\n. .\n\n.\n\n.\n\n..\n\n- .\n\n..\n\n._..\n\n. -.\n\n.\n\n.. : .\n\n..\n\n. .\n\n..\n\n.\n.\n\n.\n\n..\n\n.\n\n.. .\n\n4.0\n\nLUNAR SURFACE SCIENCE\n\nThe Apollo l u n a r surface experiments package f o r t h i s mission cons i s t e d of t h e heat flow experiment, the lunar seismic p r o f i l i n g experiment, t h e l u n a r atmospheric composition experiment, t h e lunar e j e c t a and\nmeteorites experiment, and t h e l u n a r surface gravimeter experiment. Other\nl u n a r surface experiments included the t r a v e r s e gravimeter experiment, t h e\nsurface e l e c t r i c a l p r o p e r t i e s experiment, t h e l u n a r neutron probe experiment , t h e cosmic ray d e t e c t o r experiment, the l u n a r geological investigat i o n , and t h e s o i l mechanics experiment.\nDescriptions of t h e experiment equipment or references t o documents\ni n which t h e descriptions may be found a r e contained i n Appendix A. A\ncomprehensive discussion of the preliminary s c i e n t i f i c r e s u l t s of the\nmission a r e contained i n reference 1.\n\n4.1\n\nS M A Y O LUNAR SURFACE ACTIVITIES\nU MR F\n\nThe landing point w a s i n a cratered valley between two massifs.\nFigure 4-1 i s a panoramic camera photograph of t h e Taurus-Littrow landing\ns i t e . The v a r i e t y of itopographic features at t h e Taurus-Littrow landing\ns i t e provided a valuablle a s s e t i n t h e exploration of t h e lunar surface.\nThe crew completed t h r e e periods of extravehicular a c t i v i t y during the\n75 hours on t h e surface. The events of each of t h e t h r e e periods are\nsummarized i n t a b l e 4-1 and t h e routes traversed a r e shown i n figure 4-2.\nThe arrangement of t h e experiment equipment i s shown i n figure 4-3. More\nd e t a i l e d descriptions of t h e l u n a r surface a c t i v i t i e s are provided i n\nsections 4.12 and 10.8.\n4.2\n\nAPOLLO LUNAR SURFACE EXPERIMENTS P C A E CENTRAL STATION\nAKG\n\nThe s i t e s e l e c t e d for deployment of the Apollo l u n a r surface experiments package w a s l o c a t e d approximately 185 meters west northwest (beari n g of 287O) of t h e l u n a r module ( f i g . 4-3). During preparations f o r t h e\nt r a v e r s e , t h e L u n a r Module P i l o t had d i f f i c u l t y removing t h e dome from\nt h e f u e l cask where t h e f u e l capsule i s stowed. I n s e r t i o n of the domeremoval t o o l and dome r o t a t i o n t o t h e unlocked position went smoothly;\nhowever, t h e t o o l e x t r a c t i o n p u l l r e s u l t e d i n a separation of the t o o l\nfrom t h e dome. However,\nt h e noise f r o m t h e guidance and navigation system w a s i n c o r r e c t l y pred i c t e d and a reassessment of t h e guidance and navigation noise l e v e l i s\nrequired before an accurate assessment of the g a l a c t i c noise can be made.\nThe 24 hours of d a t a collected during t r m s e a r t h coast will be used t o\nanalyze t e r r e s t r i a l noise sources and improve t h e c a l i b r a t i o n of the antenna p a t t e r n .\nMinor problems were encountered with operation of the equipment and\nf i l m processing. The f i l m recorded occzsional s t a t i c discharge marks as\na r e s u l t of s t a t i c e l e c t r i c i t y generated when the f i l m edge rubbed against\nt h e r o l l e r flange. Also, streaks and flow patterns were noted and appare n t l y were acquired during the f i l m development process. The nature and\nspacing of t h e discharge and development marks had l i t t l e e f f e c t on the\ndata.\nRandom dark bands\'were observed on the film but coherent data existed\nbetween t h e bands. Thk bands are believed t o have r e s u l t e d from arcing\ni n t h e high voltage poher supply for the recorder cathode ray display tube.\nT h i s problem w a s observed at turn-on dzring p r e f l i g h t vacuum t e s t i n g of the\nApollo 17 recorder. Data were obtained both while t h e command and service module\nwas i n l u n a r o r b i t and during t r a n s e a r t h coast with a t o t a l of 164 hours\nand 45 d n u t e s of operating t i m e . Data were obtained i n t h e u l t r a v i o l e t\nspectrum region (1180 t o 1680 angstroms). Observations were accomplished\nof t h e l u n a r s u r f a c e , lunar atmosphere, zodiacal l i g h t , and g a l a c t i c and\ne x t r a g a l a c t i c sources.\nInstrument performance w a s normal except f o r a background count r a t e\nt h a t w a s higher than expected. The higher count r a t e , which has been tent a t i v e l y a t t r i b u t e d t o cosmic r a d i a t i o n , raises t h e lower l i m i t f o r detect i o n of weak s i g n a l s , but does not a f f e c t the precision with which stronger\nl i g h t s i g n a l s can be measured. The high count r a t e a l s o does not a f f e c t\nt h e absolute s e n s i t i v i t y of t h e spectrometer.\nO t h e l a s t day of operation of the u l t r a v i o l e t spectrometer, two\nn\ntelemetered temperature measurements ceased t o operate. Section 15.5.4\ncontains a discussion of t h i s problem. This anomaly did not i n any manner a f f e c t t h e a b i l i t y o f , t h e spectrometer t o complete i t s mission.\nA portion of t h e s p e d t r a l data has been evaluated, and some scient i f i c observations a r e presented i n t h e following paragraphs.\n\nThe t o t a l l u n a r atmosphere appears t o be much l e s s dense than t h e\nmaximum p a r t i c l e density of l o 7 per cubic centimeter reported by the\nApollo 1 4 cold cathode pressure gage. The Apollo 17 mission u l t r a v i o l e t\nspectrometer w a s s e n s i t i v e t o all possible atmospheric constituents except argon, helium, and neon. The upper l i m i t t o t h e t o t a l p a r t i c l e\ndensity of all constituents observed on the Ayollo 17 mission i s l o 5 per\ncubic centimeter.\nOnly atomic hydrogen w a s p o s i t i v e l y i d e n t i f i e d i n the lunar atmosphere. The p a r t i c l e density-was less than 50 per cubic centimeter, which\ni s about 1 percent of t h e density expected, i f solar-wind protons undergo\na charge exchange at t h e surface and t h e m a l l y escape.\nLunar albedo measurements confirm laboratory measurements performed\nwith lunar samples from previous missions. The f l i g h t data show t h e same\nangular s c a t t e r i n g function observed i n the visible region. Albedo varia t i o n s and t h e i r r e l a t i o n s h i p t o t h e lunar geography are being analyzed.\nNew information about the s o l a r atmosphere (including zodiacal l i g h t )\nand t h e e a r t h \xe2\x80\x99 s atmosphere was obtained during t h e mission. Additionally ,\nvery good d a t a were obtained during t r a n s e a r t h coast on s t e l l a r and ext r a g a l a c t i c sources , and a general u l t r a v i o l e t slqr survey w a s conducted.\n\n..\n,\n\n,\n.. .\n\n.\n\n.\n-...<..\n\n.\n\n.\n\n.\n\n\'\n\n.\n\n.\n\n.. . .\n\n-\n\n.\n\n-\n\n-.\n\n_.\n\n. ..\n\n.\n\n5-5\n\n5.4\n\nINFRARED SCANNIliG RADIOMETER\n\nThe i n f r a r e d scanning radiometer (S-171) performed normally during\nall l u n a r o r b i t phases of t h e mission and during t r a n s e a r t h coast. Photography\n\nThe one s o l a r corona photographic sequence obtained consisted of 7\nphotographs w i t h exposure times 1 0 , 4 , 1, 1 / 2 , 1/8, 1/30, and 1/60-second\ns t a r t i n g 75 seconds before sunrise and ending 1 0 seconds p r i o r t o sunrise.\nThe sequence w a s made using t h e Hasselblzd e l e c t r i c camera with the 80-m\nlens set a t f / 2 . 8 , and high-speed, black-and-white f i l m .\nThe sunrise coronal sequence provides data on t h e e a s t limb of the\nsun. J u p i t e r appears i n all seven of the photographs, thereby perinitt i n g s i m p l i f i e d indexing and t r a c i n g of the data. Two coronal streamers\nare evident i n t h e photograph taken 6.4 seconds p r i o r t o sunrise; and\nwhile these streamers seem t o appear i n the photograph taken 30 seconds\np r i o r t o s\'mrise , photographic techniques and microdensitometry will be\nrequired t o determine t h e i r f u l l extent. One streamer l i e s nearly along\nt h e e c l i p t i c , and t h e excellent sketches made by t h e crew during the miss i o n as w e l l as t h e i r descriptions during t h e debriefings show and include\n\n..\n\n.\n\n.-\n\n..\n\n.\n\n. .\n\n. :..\n,\n\n..\n\n. _\n\n5-14\nstreamers extending along t h e e c l i p t i c out t o J u p i t e r , some 24.4 degrees\nfrom t h e center of t h e sun. Microdensitometry traces w i l l be made from\nt h e l u n a r limb out along t h e e c l i p t i c on all photographs t o determine the\nbrightness of t h e corona a s a function of distance from t h e center of t h e\nSun.\n\nZodiacal l i g h t photography data were obtained with greater success\non t h i s inission than on any previous attempt, due primarily t o refined\nprocedures and equipment. The zodiacal l i g h t extending eastward from t h e\nlunar-occulted sun was recorded i n t h r e e separate s e r i e s of photographs.\nEach of t h e s e pre-sunrise s e r i e s included overlapping frames covering\nelongation angles from 80 degrees t o 0.5 degree eastward along the eclipt i c plane. The 35-mm camera system w a s used with t h e 55-mm l e n s a t f/1.2,\nand front-end ( c o l o r o r p o l a r i z i n g ) f i l t e r s using high-speed, black-andwhite f i l m . The camera w a s bracket-mounted behind t h e right-hand rendezvous window and w a s pointed almost p a r a l l e l t o the vehicle plus X axis\nwhen color f i l t e r s were i n use, and 30 degrees from t h e plus X a x i s towards t h e minus Z a x i s when t h e l a r g e r polarizing f i l t e r was i n use. For\nt h e zodiacal l i g h t t a s k s , t h e camera\'s o p t i c a l axis w a s i n i t i a l l y pointed\ni n t h e d i r e c t i o n of t h e vehicle instantaneous velocity vector by properly\no r i e n t i n g t h e spacecraft. T h i s allowed t h e camera a x i s t o be aimed close\nt o t h e e c l i p t i c plane with one corner of the frame viewing a portion of\nt h e l u n a r limb c u t t i n g across t h e e c l i p t i c . S t e l l a r images on the data\nframes provide good postf;ight references f o r pointing accuracies.\nThe f i r s t s e r i e s of ll d a t a frames was taken i n red f i l t e r e d l i g h t\non revolution 23. An exposure time of 90 seconds was used i n i t i a l l y f o r\npositions f a r t h e s t away from t h e sun, and the exposure t i m e w a s decreased\nas t h e zodiacal l i g h t \' s brightness increased, so t h a t t h e f i n a l exposure\nw a s 1/30 second within 0.5 degree of the sun.\nA second s e r i e s of photographs was made i n blue f i l t e r e d l i g h t on\nrevolution 28. Since it used t h e same Siming and duration of exposures\nas t h e previous s e r i e s , a d i r e c t frame-for-frame comparison w i l l show\ns p e c t r a l content differences. Both s e r i e s w i l l a l s o be compared d i r e c t l y\nt o matching duration exposures of a calibrated stepwedge, illuminated by\na s o l a r equivalent l i g h t source. Calibration exposures were c a r r i e d on\nt h e f l i g h t s o t h a t they experienced t h e same l e v e l of r a d i a t i o n fogging\nas t h e data frames, and t h e i r combined analysis w i l l remove emulsion reci p r o c i t y f a i l u r e e f f e c t s from t h e f i n a l photometric results.\n\nThe polarized series was composed of eleven sets of photographs over\ncorresponding regions of t h e sky. This was flown on Apollo 17 t o obtain a measure of induced c r y s t a l a c t i v a t i o n from primary protons and secondary neut r o n s while i n t h e spacecraft. The induced a c t i v i t y w a s believed t o be a\nmajor source of i n t e r f e r e n c e i n t h e g a l a c t i c flux data taken on Apollo 15\nand 16.\n\n.\n\n_\n\nI\n\n.-- ..-.\n.\n,..:.\'.,.-..._\n.\n:\n.\n~\n\n.. ..\n\n.,:\n;.\n\nThe a c t i v e p a r t of t h e experiment s t a r t e d a f t e r landing and recovery\nof t h e c r y s t a l from t h e spacecraft. An ident i c a l c r y s t a l not flown aboard Apollo 17 was used as a control throughout\nt h e measurement program.\nA t t h e t i m e of p u b l i c a t i o n , q u a l i t a t i v e i d e n t i f i c a t i o n s have been made\nof t h e following nuclear i s o t o p e s ; Sodium 24, Iodine 123, Iodine 124, 10d i n e 125, Iodine 126, and possible Antimony 1 2 4 and Sodium 22. Results\ni n d i c a t e t h a t t h e induced a c t i v i t y can be a t t r i b u t e d mainly t o species with\nhalf l i v e s of about half a day and longer. Significant i n t e n s i t i e s of decay products with s h o r t e r h a l f l i v e s have not been seen, although i f such\nproducts were present they should have been observed.\n\nThe f i n a l r e s u l t s of t h i s experiment w i l l be reported i n a supplement\nt o t h i s report.\n\n5.11 APOLLO WINDOW METEOROID EXPERIMENT\nThis Apollo window meteoroid experiment (s-176) u t i l i z e s t h e comand\nmodule s i d e and hatch windows f o r detecting meteoroids having a mass of\ngrams or l e s s . The b i o s t a c k experiment (M-211) was conducted t o determine t h e biol o g i c a l e f f e c t s of heavy i o n s from cosmic sources and t h e space f l i g h t\nenvironment on p l a n t and animal b i o l o g i c a l systems i n a dormant s t a t e .\nThe experiment was h e r m e t i c a l l y s e a l e d a d self-contained.\nThe b i o s t a c k c o n t a i n e r l i k e t h e one flown on Apollo 1 6 , was r e t u r n e d\nt o t h e p r i n c i p a l i n v e s t i g a t o r i n Frankfurt/Main Germany and opened within\n10 days a f t e r landing. The r e s u l t s w i l l be published i n a supplement t o\nt h i s report.\n6.2\n\n_- .\n__ -..\n- -,.\n\n.\n\nBIOLOGICAL COSMIC RADIATION EXPERIMENT\n\nThe b i o l o g i c a l cosmic r a d i a t i o n e e e r i m e n t was a passive experiment\nt o determine if damage could be detected i n t h e b r a i n and eyes of pocket\nmice after exposure t o heavy cosmic p a r t i c l e s i n t h e s p a c e c r a f t cabin environment. A dosimeter recorded t h e l e v e l of r a d i a t i o n i n t h e v i c i n i t y\nof t h e experiment and two temperature recorders i n d i c a t e d t h e maximum and\nminimum temperatures w i t h i n t h e c a n i s t e r . The experiment package w a s removed from t h e s p a c e c r a f t about 4 1 / 2 hours a f t e r landing. The i n t e r n a l\np r e s s u r e o f 1 0 . 1 p s i a w a s immediately r s i s e d t o 14.7 p s i a with a 1-to-1\nmixture o f oxygen and helium. The c a n i s t e r w a s then flown t o t h e Lyndon\nB. Johnson T r o p i c a l Medical Center i n American Samoa. The experiment w a s\nopened approximately 7 1 / 2 hours a f t e r landing and 4 mice were observed\nmoving about i n t h e i r t u b u l a r compartments. The f i f t h mouse w a s dead\nand i t s death w a s not caused by r a d i a t i o n . The p h y s i c a l condition of t h e\nl i v i n g mice appeared t o be e x c e l l e n t . The recorders i n d i c a t e d t h a t t h e\ntemperature w i t h i n t h e c a n i s t e r ranged from 71.0\' F t o 85.7\' F. During p o s t f l i g h t\npurging of t h e oxidizer manifold, t h e plus p i t c h and plus yaw engines o f\nt h e command module reaction control system 1 responded simultaneously t o\nground support equipment commands. The problem was located i n t h e ground\nsupport equipment and t h a t hardware w i l l be modified for the Skylab\nprogram.\n\n7.9\n\nENVIRONMENTAL CONTROL SYSTEM\n\nThe environmental control system provided an acceptable environment\nf o r t h e crew and spacecraft equipment. During t h e d a i l y water chlorinat i o n operations, leakage was observed i n and around t h e casing assembly\nof t h e i n j e c t o r mechanism! Despite t h e minor leakage, a l l chlorination\nprocedures were accomplished as scheduled. This anomaly i s discussed\ni n s e c t i o n 15.1.6.\nDuring four e a r l y lunar o r b i t s , t h e water/glycol temperature control\nvalve (mixing valve) f a i l e d t o open properly as t h e r a d i a t o r o u t l e t temperature decreased. The mixed coolant temperature momentarily f e l l as\nmuch as 4\' F below t h e s p e c i f i c a t i o n control band of 42\' t o 48\' F during\nmixing s t a r t u p . N corrective action w a s taken and i n i t i a t i o n of mixing\no\nw a s proper during all subsequent lunar o r b i t s and during t r a n s e a r t h coast\nThis anomaly i s discussed i n section 15.1.10.\nThe r a d i a t o r flow proportioning valve automatically switched over\nt o t h e backup control system f o r t h e primary coolant loop. About 4 hours\nl a t e r , t h e crew r e s e t t h e flow c o n t r o l t o the primary system and returned\ncontrol t o t h e automatic mode a f t e r which operation of t h e valve was sati s f a c t o r y . Switching normally occurs f o r any one of t h e following conditions.\n\na. The indicated temperature difference between t h e two r a d i a t o r\n5\npanels i s g r e a t e r than 1\' F and the flow control valve i s not drawing\nfull current.\n\n-.\n-- -\n\nI-\n\ni.\n\n..\n\n.\n\n- ,\n\n.\n\n.\n\n. . _ , ....\n\n, . .\n\n7-9\nb. The systems discrepancies and anomalies a r e mentioned i n\nt h i s section and discussed i n g r e a t e r d e t a i l i n section 15.2.\n\n8.1 STRUCTURE3 AND MEXKUICU SYSTEMS\nA l mechanical systems functioned properly. The loads at landing\nl\nwere low based on t h e r a t e of descent at footpad contact of about 3 f t / s e c .\nThe vehicle a t t i t u d e on the lunar surface was approximately 5.3 degrees\np i t c h up with a l e f t roll of about 2.6 degrees.\n8.2\n\nELECTRICAL P W R DISTRIBUTION AND BATTERIES\nO E\n1\n\nThe e l e c t r i c a l power system performed as expected.\n\nThe descent batt e r i e s delivered 1585 [ampere-hours out of a nominal t o t a l capacity of\n2075 w e r e - h o u r s\nA t j e t t i s o n , t h e ascent b a t t e r i e s , had delivered about\n300 ampere-hours out of a normal capacity of 592 w e r e - h o u r s , and at imp a c t , over 200 ampere-hours remained. The dc bus voltage was maintained\na t about 28.8 v o l t s , and the maximum observed current was 71 amperes duri n g t h e powered descent.\n\n.\n\n8.3\n\nCOMMUNICATIONS\n\nA l l functions of t h e communications system were acceptable during\neach phase of t h e mission. The S-band steerable antenna l o s t lock seve r a l times because of vehicle blockage o r reaching t h e gimbal limits. No\nl o s s e s of automatic t r a c k , due t o divergent antenna o s c i l l a t i o n s , were\nnoted as experienced on several previous missions.\n\n8.4 INSTRUMENTATION\nThe instrumentation system operated normally except f o r a b a t t e r y 4\nmeasurement s h i f t which w a s noted at about 108 hours. This anomaly is\ndiscussed i n section 15.2.1.\n\n. ..\n. . _.\n.\n.\n\n. ......*\n- .\n\n.\n\n.\n\n.\n\n.\n\n8- 2\n\n8.5\n\nRADAR\n\nLanding radar performance was normal during powered descent. Velocity\na c q u i s i t i o n was obtained at an estimated a l t i t u d e of 42 000 f e e t , p r i o r t o\nchanging t h e lunar module yaw a t t i t u d e from 70 t o 20 degrees. Range acq u i s i t i o n was obtained during t h e yaw maneuver, at an a l t i t u d e of approximately 39 000 f e e t . Antenna position and r a g e s c a l e change occurred at\nt h e predicted t i m e and tracking w a s continuous t o lunar touchdown. There\nwas no lock-up on moving dust or debris n e a t h e lunar surface.\nThe rendezvous radar performance w a s normal f o r a l l mission phases\nincluding s e l f - t e s t , rendezvous radar/transponder checkout , and rendezvous tracking.\n\n8.6\n\nDESCENT PROPULSION SYSTEM\n\nThe descent propulsion system performed s a t i s f a c t o r i l y . The t o t a l\ntime f o r t h e descent f i r i n g was approximately 727 seconds. The propellant\nquantity gaging system indicated about 1225 l b of usable propellant remaining a t engine shutdown. This i s equivalent t o 117 seconds of hover t i m e .\nThe descent propulsion syTtem pressures and temperatures were as expected\nduring all phases of the mission. The s u p e r c r i t i c a l helium system functioned normally. Performance of both t h e s u p e r c r i t i c a l helium tank and\nt h e ambient s t a r t b o t t l e i s shown i n t a b l e 8-1.\nThe gaging probe readings at engine shutdown are given i n t a b l e 8-11.\nThe low-level sensor w a s a c t i v a t e d at lunar landing and w a s probably a res u l t of propellant s l o s h , since a l l probes indicated propellant q u a n t i t i e s\nabove t h e sensor a c t i v a t i o n l e v e l .\n\n8.7 ASCENT PROPULSION SYSTEM\nThe ascent propulsion system performance was s a t i s f a c t o r y f o r t h e\nl u n a r ascent and a terminal phase i n i t i a t i o n f i r i n g s . The engine f i r i n g\nduration f o r t h e ascent maneuver was 441 seconds. The terminal phase i n i t i a t i o n maneuver f i r i n g t i m e w a s approximatelly 2.7 seconds, however, no\nd a t a were received during t h e second f i r i n g . System pressures and tempera t u r e s were normal during all phases of the nission.\n\n1\n\ni\n\n. . . . . ._\n\n.\n\n, - d\n\n......\n\n....\n\nrrr;rrcr.ii?-?-\xe2\x80\x9d.\n\n.............. -.\n\n.-.!<\n.-\n\n8- 3\n\nTABLE 8 -I.- DESCENT PROPULSION S S E\nYT M\nSUPERCRITICAL HELIUM SYSTEM P R O M N E\nEFR AC\n\n~~\n\nValue\n\nPar m e t e r\nS u p e r c r i t i c a l helium b o t t l e\nPressure r i s e r a t e from l i f t - o f f t o\npowered descent maneuver i g n i t i o n\n\n........\n\n6.8 p s i / h r\n\nPressures during powered descent\n\n...............\nPeak pressure . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .\nPressure at l u n a r touchdown . . . . . . . . . .\nI g n i t i o n pressure\n\n1304 p s i a\n\n1415 p s i a\n482 p s i a\n\nAmbient s t a r t b o t t l e\nPressure l e v e l from loading t o use\nI\nI\n\nPressure a f t e r squib valve opening\n\n.......\n\n1646 t o\n1625 p s i a\n\n.......\n\n....\n............\n\n600 p s i a\n602 p s i a\n\nPressure at i g n i t i o n f o r powered descent\nPressure at l u n a r landing\n\nTABLE 8-11.-\n\n.\n\n602 p s i a\n\nPROPELLANT QUANTITY GAGING SYSTEM DATA\nAT ENGINE SHUTDOWN\n\nTank 2\n\nTotal\n\nOxidi zer\n\n......\n.........\n\n6.6\n379\n\n7.1\n\n---\n\n407\n\n796\n\nQuantity, percent\n\n6.4\n\n6.2\n\n---\n\nWeight, lb\n\n......\n.........\n\n230\n\n223\n\n453\n\nQuantity, percent\nWeight, l b\nFuel\n\n. . .\n\n.\n.- ... ...-.:\n\n.i.:\n\nb\n\n. .-\n\n.\n\n-\n\n..-.\n\n-\n\n. .\n\n.. .\n\n.\n\n.\n\n.\n\n-\n\n~\n\n. .\nC\n\n..\n\n.\n\n.\n\n8-4\n8.8 GUIDANCE , NAVIGATION , AND CONTROL\nThe performance of t h e primary guidance, navigation, and control syst e m as well as t h e abort guidance system was normal throughout the mission. Table 9-1 shows the performance parameters from t h e l u nar roving vehicle. C o n t r o l l a b i l i t y was good, and no problems were experienced with s t e e r i n g , braking, or obstacle negotiation. The navigation\nsystem gyro d r i f t and closure e r r o r at the lunar module were negligible.\nA l l i n t e r f a c e s between t h e crew and the lunar roving vehicle and between\nt h e lunar roving vehicle and the stowed payload were s a t i s f a c t o r y . A det a i l e d discussion of t h e l u n a r roving vehicle performance i s contained i n\nreference 2.\nDeployment of t h e l u n a r roving vehicle from the l u n a r module was\nsmooth and no s i g n i f i c a n t problems were encountered. The chassis lock\npins d i d not s e a t f u l l y , but t h e crew used the deployment assist t o o l t o\ns e a t the pins.\nA t i n i t i a l power,-up, t h e lunar roving vehicle b a t t e r y temperatures\nwere higher than predicted; 95\' F f o r battery 1 and 110\xc2\xb0F f o r b a t t e r y 2\ncompared t o the predikted temperatures of 80\' F f o r each. This w a s part i a l l y due t o t h e t r a n s l u n a r a t t i t u d e p r o f i l e flown, and p a r t i a l l y t o a\nbias i n t h e b a t t e r y temperature meter. Following adequate b a t t e r y cooldown a f t e r t h e f i r s t extravehicular a c t i v i t y , temperatures f o r the remainder of t h e l u n a r surface operations were about as predicted.\nThe following lunar roving vehicle systems problems were noted:\na. The b a t t e r y 2 temperature indication w a s off-scale low a t t h e\ns t a r t of t h e t h i r d extravehicular a c t i v i t y .\nb. The r i g h t r e a r fender extension was knocked o f f p r i o r t o leaving\nt h e l u n a r module on t h e f i r s t extravehicular traverse.\nThe b a t t e r y 2 temperature indication was off-scale low at the beginning of t h e t h i r d extravehicular a c t i v i t y . This condition continued f o r\nt h e remainder of t h e l u n a r surface operation. The most probable cause\nw a s a shorted thermistor i n t h e battery. The same condition was noted on\nground t e s t i n g of two o t h e r b a t t e r i e s . Electrolyte leakage through t h e\nsensor bond, as a result of elevated temperatures, may have caused t h e\ns h o r t . Temperature monitoring was continued using b a t t e r y 1 as an indic a t o r with temperature trends established from d a t a on the f i r s t and second extravehicular a c t i v i t i e s f o r b a t t e r y 2.\n\n\\o\n\nI\n\nTABLE 9-1.- LUNAR ROVING VEHICLE PERFORMANCE PARAMETERS\n\nN\n\n~~\n\nFirst\nextravehicula\nactivity\n\n.........\nM p distance, kilometers . . . . . .\na\naOdometer distance, kilometers . . . .\nTraverse . . . . . . . . . . . . .\nAdditional . . . . . . . . . . . .\nDrive time, minutes\n\nSecond\nextravehicular\nactivity\n138.5\n\n27\n\n88\n\nTotal\n\nMission\nplanning\nvalue\n\n253.5\n\nThird\nextravehicular\nactivity\n\n280\n\n2.3\n\n19.0\n\n11.0\n\n2.5\n1.0\n\n20.2\n\n12.0\n\n0.2\n\n0.1\n\n...........\n\n8.25\n\n8.20\n\n7.6\n\n5.1\n\n-\n\n-.\n\n32.5\n\n36.0\n\nbTraverse mobility r a t e ,\nkilometers/hr\n\n32.3\n\n37; The\ncrew had no d i f f i c u l t y i n donning o r doffing the suits and t h e portable\nl i f e support systems operated s a t i s f a c t o r i l y .\nSystem operation during i n t e g r i t y checks of t h e extravehicular mob i l i t y u n i t s p r i o r t o each extravehicular a c t i v i t y w a s normal. The tanks, however, did not\nrequire any a d d i t i o n a l water. Emphasis has been placed upon t h e operational and hardware differences from previous f l i g h t s w i t h descriptions and opinions presented on\nphases o f t h e f l i g h t t h a t were deemed operationally i n t e r e s t i n g . The procedures employed were the same as\n\n10-33 \n\n\nFigure 10-16.- Commander place the traverse gravimeter\non the lunar surface.\nfor all previous service propulsion system firings. Mission rules dic\xc2\xad\ntated that the lunar module crew be informed of the successful circulari\xc2\xad\nzation maneuver prior to their initiation of the second descent orbit in\xc2\xad\nsertion maneuver. Simplex A VHF communications were maintained and the\nCommand Module Pilot utilized the voice-operated relay mode for transmit\xc2\xad\nting to ~he lunar module. A timeline difference between the Apollo 17\nmission and previous missions was that the inertial measurement unit\nrealignment/crew optical alignment sight calibration was delayed until\nafter the circularization firing because this task was originally within\nthe time frame for lunar module separation. The Command Module Pilot time\xc2\xad\nline for 7 to 8 hours following lunar module separation was not overly\n\n10-34\n\nFigure 10-17.- Lunar surface electrical properties receiver \n\non lunar roving vehicle. \n\ncrowded; however, there was no scheduled eating period during this time,\nand activity prevented suit removal.\n10.9.2\n\nVisual Observations\n\nThe human observer\'s capability to describe and interpret what is be\xc2\xad\ning observed greatly enhances all existing, or proposed, imaging devices.\nThe human eye can see and identify geological features which either fail\nto appear or which appear, but can not be recognized on photographs. This\nis particularly true when attempting to describe subtle color differences.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n10-35\n\nFigure 10-18.- Surface electrical properties transmitter\nshowing taped panels.\nThe eye can determine the subtle color differences much better than any\nonboard photographic device that was available. Another advantage of the\nhuman eye is the ability to almost simultaneously observe features in a\nwide range of illumination. In the low sun-angle observations, the eye\ncould see detail in the shadows. This is primarily due to reflected il\xc2\xad\nlumination from the other side of the craters; however, detail in shadow\nalmost never shows in the photographs. Good viewing of a particular spot\non the lunar surface is limited to about 1 minute. The observer must,\ntherefore, develop the capability to observe and describe at the same\ntime. When utilizing the command module as a visual observation platform,\nthere are some compromises necessary; however, placement of the windows\nis such that usually one or two windows are directed toward the lunar\n\n10-36\nsurface . The technique of observation and simultaneously using a hand\xc2\xad\nheld camera requires that the crewman stabilize his body with his feet\nbecause both hands are always busy manipulating the camera. In making\nvisual observations, it is easy to position the body as required to ob\xc2\xad\nserve a particular target. Surface target recognition is more readily\naccomplished in vehicle attitudes which allow observation as the space\xc2\xad\ncraft approaches the target. Forward viewing along the velocity vector\nprovides rapid target recognition and also permits increased time for\nobservation. Conversely, attitudes which do not allow target recogni\xc2\xad\ntion until the spacecraft is either over, or has passed beyond the tar\xc2\xad\nget, provide the least amount of time for useful observation.\nThe importance of establishing a preflight visual observation plan\nand then following it throughout the flight cannot be overstressed. This\nwas accomplished on the Apollo 17 mission by assigning 10 specific visual\ntargets. The visual targets were well planned preflight, and all visual\ntargets were discussed during the course of the mission.\nThe moon is a very intriguing body and the little spare time was\nspent observing or just looking out the windows and picking out unusual\ngeological characteristics, descriptions of which were recorded on the\ndata storage equipment tape or related over the air-to-ground network.\nPreflight geological training for visual observation was thorough. The\npreflight training must be thorough enough to enable instantaneous recall\nof the general geography of the lunar surface. The effectiveness of the\nobservational techniques can be improved by the use of optical devices\nwith image stabilization and greater magnification.\nThe Apollo 17 landing site was unique in that the greatest intensity\nof earth illumination, or earthshine, was available during this mission.\nVisual observations in earthshine must be restricted to. differences in\nalbedo characteristics; crater rims are very visible, and the slopes and\nslumping associated with the crater walls can be seen in the larger cra\xc2\xad\nters. The ejected ray material is not visible for more than about a\nquarter of the crater diameter beyond the crater. However, anything that\nshows up as an albedo difference in sunshine shows up as an albedo dif\xc2\xad\nference in earthshine. The illumination of the lunar surface in earth\xc2\xad\nshine is brighter than the brightest moonlit night observed on earth. An\natt~mpt was made to use the onboard color wheel to determine colors and\nalbedos of the lunar surface; however, none of the color chips approached\nthe actual colors on the moon. Any further attempts to label the colors\nof the moon should be accomplished with a color wheel that varies from\nvery light tan to light browns. It should also have the various colors\nof light gray to blue-gray and tan-gray to a darkish gray. One big prob\xc2\xad\nlem of utilizing a color wheel is that the color wheel should be exposed\nto the same light as the object on the lunar surface.\n\nA t h i n cloud of s m a l l p a r t i c l e s could always be seen following t h e\nspacecraft from spacecraft sunrise and sunset t o t h e l u n a r surface termin a t o r crossing. These p a r t i c l e s had the same general appearance of waste\nwater d r o p l e t s and would show up as a bright b l u r i n t h e sextant. A t tempts t o observe them with the binoculars gave e s s e n t i a l l y t h e same appearance as t h e unaided eye. They were assumed t o be i c e p a r t i c l e s and\nwere always with t h e spacecraft. During the waste water dumps , they were\no r i g i n a l l y very dense, but would l a t e r disseminate t o a wider area. This\np a r t i c l e concentration w a s r e l a t i v e l y low; however , it remained constant\na f t e r a steady-state period was reache? following t h e waste water dumps.\n\n_ .\n10.9.3\n\nPhotography\n\nOne s u n r i s e s o l a r corona photograDhic sequence and t h r e e sunrise zod i c a l l i g h t photographic sequences were accomplished. The t h r e e\nastronauts accumulated 913.5 man-hours of space f l i g h t experience during\nt h e 12.6-day mission. The Commander and L u n a r Module P i l o t each accumul a t e d approximately 22 hours of extravehicular a c t i v i t y time on t h e lunar\nsurface and t h e Command Module P i l o t accumulated 1 hour and 6 minutes of\nextravehicular a c t i v i t y during the transearth coast phase. All i n f l i g h t\nmedical o b j e c t i v e s were successfully coigleted. A l l physiological parame t e r s remained within t h e expected ranges, and no medically s i g n i f i c a n t\narrhythmias occurred during t h e mission.\n\nTwo b i o l o g i c a l experiments and a l i g h t f l a s h investigation were conducted during t h e mission t o gather datz on s o l a r radiation e f f e c t s on\nb i o l o g i c a l systems. Weightlessness\n\nTwo of t h e t h r e e crewmen experienced t h e t y p i c a l f\'ullness-of-thehead sensation , one immediately a r t e r earth-orbital i n s e r t i o n and t h e\no t h e r a f % e r a 6-hour exposure t o weightlessness.\n\nBased on t h e crew\'s p o s t f l i g h t comments, t h e adaptation t o weightl e s s n e s s required approximately 1 1 / 2 days. There were no instances of\nnausea, vomiting, o r d i s o r i e n t a t i o n ; however, a l l t h r e e crewmen d i d experience t h e need t o l i m i t t h e i r movements and perform t h e necessary movements slowly during t h e i n i t i a l period of adeptation. I n addition, all\nt h r e e crewmen had varying degrees of "stomech awareness" and a decreased\na p p e t i t e f o r t h e f i r s t 1 1 / 2 days of f l i g h t . Once adapted, t h e crewmen\nwere able t o perform all types of movements without r e s t r i c t i o n s . No\nreadaptation t o weightlessness was required a f t e r r e s i d i n g f o r 75 hours\non t h e moon at 1/6-g.\n\n.\n\n. .\n*-.. ...\n"\n.\n\n_ .\nI\n\n1\n\n\\\n\n:.. -.\n. ...\n\n..I\nI\n.\n\ni\n. .\n:\n\nI\n.\n\n.\n\n...~\n\n11-11\n\n11.2.2\n\nMedicztions\n\nThe medications taken by each crewman a r e presented i n t h e following\ntable :\nU n i t s taken\n\nMedi c a t ion\nCommander\n\nSeconal\n(Sleep 1\nSirnethicone\n(Antiflatulence)\n\nCommand\nModule\nPilot\n\nLunar\nModule\nPilot\n\n3\n29\n\nDexedrine scopolamine\n(Antimotion sickness )\n\n1\n\nAspirin\n(Headache )\n\n0\n\nI\nLomot i1\n(htiperistdsi!s)\n\n0\n\nAfrin nose drops\n(Decongestant )\n\n0\n\nActifed\n(Decongestant )\n\n0\n\nMore medications were taken on t h i s f l i g h t than on any previous mission.\nThe i n t e r m i t t e n t use of Seconal for sleep by all t h r e e crewmen and t h e\nd a i l y use of Simethicone f o r symptomatic r e l i e f of g a s t r o i n t e s t i n a l f l a t ulence by one of t h e crewmen were the p r i n c i p a l f a c t o r s contributing t o\nt h e high intake of medications.\nThe Command Module P i l o t and the L u n a r Module P i l o t experienced one\nloose bowel movement each, on t h e eleventh and twelfth days of f l i g h t ,\nrespectively. I n addition, changes t o t h e f l i g h t\nplan occasionally impacted previously planned crew s l e e p periods. I n\ngeneral, however, an adequate amount of good sleep w a s obtained by all\ncrewmembers. The estimates of s l e e p duration made by ground personnel\nwere i n general agreement w i t h t h e crew\'s subjective evaluations.\nA l l t h r e e crewmen averaged approximately s i x hours of s l e e p per day\nthroughout t h e mission. Only during t h e f i r s t sleep period w a s t h e amount\nof s l e e p obtained (approximately t h r e e hours) inadequate from a medical\npoint of v i e w .\n\nThe crew reported t h a t t h e Seconal e f f e c t i v e l y induced sound and undisturbed s l e e p f o r a period of four t o f i v e hours. Sleep r e s t r a i n t s were\nused f o r every s l e e p period by all t h r e e crewmen. The Commander a l s o emphasized t h e importance of programming an eight-hour sleep period each day.\n\ni\nI\n\n11.2.4\n\nRadiation\n\nThe personal r a d i a t i o n dosimeters showed the t o t a l absorbed dose t o\nt h e crew w a s s l i g h t l y l e s s than 0.6 rad at skin depth. T h i s i s well below\nt h e t h r e s h o l d of detectable medical e f f e c t s .\nThe L u n a r Module P i l o t \' s personal radiation dosimeter read higher\nthan t h e o t h e r two crewmens\' u n i t s , due t o ety-pical performance of t h e\ndosimeter at low dose r a t e s .\n\nll.2.5\n\nCardiac Arrhythmias\n\nN medically s i g n i f i c a n t arrhythmias occurred during t h e mission,\no\nbut i s o l a t e d premature h e a r t beats were observed i n two of t h e t h r e e crewmen. The f a c t t h a t t h e frequency (less than one per day) and character\nof t h e s e p r e m a t u r i t i e s remained consistent with electrocardiographic d a t a\nobtained on t h e s e same crewmen during ground-based t e s t s , c l e a r l y indicates t h a t they were not r e l a t e d t o or r e s u l t a n t from space f l i g h t . I n\naddition., one crewman demonstrated t e n i s o l a t e d i n t e r m i t t e n t contractions\nof t h e a u r i c l e s t h a t were not transmitted t o the v e n t r i c l e s (non-conducted\nP-waves).\nThis benign conduction defect was associated with an increased\ndischarge of t h e vagus nerve (vagotonia). The Apollo 1 7 mission was the most productive m d trouble-free\nmanned mission. This represents t h e cdmination of continual advancements i n hardware, procedures , t r a i n i n g , planning , operations , and scient i f i c experiments.\n2. The Apollo 17 f l i g h t demonstrrted t h e p r a c t i c a l i t y of t r a i n i n g\ns c i e n t i s t s t o become q u a l i f i e d astronmxLs and yet r e t a i n t h e i r e x p e r t i s e\nand knowledge i n t h e s c i e n t i f i c f i e l d .\n\n3. S t a r s and t h e horizon are not v i s i b l e during night launches ,\nt h e r e f o r e out-of-the-window alignment techniques cannot be used f o r att i t u d e reference.\n\n. ..\n\n:\n\n4. A-15), flown for the first time, was a ra\xc2\xad\ndiation experiment using mice. The hardware consisted of a hermatically\nsealed, cylindrical aluminum canister that contained seven perforated,\ncylindrical tubes. Attached to one end of the canister were redundant\npressure relief valves and two manually controlled purge valves. Six of\nthe seven tubes were arranged around the inside wall of the canister. The\nseventh tube was centrally located in the six-tube circular arrangement.\nFive of the six tubes contained a mouse and its food supply. The sixth\ntube was flown empty because the oxygen generating capability of the en\xc2\xad\nvironmental control system indicated a marginal supply of oxygen for 6\nmice. The tube in the center contained potassium superoxide granules\nfor life support and operated by converting the carbon dioxide from the\nmice into oxygen. A radiation dosimeter was implanted beneath the scalp\nof each mouse, and a separate dosimeter was included in the stowage locker\n\nFigure A-15.- Biological cosmic radiation experiment hardware.\n\nA-26\nnext t o t h e hardware.
